<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Sudden movement of the Earth's crust</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Earthquake_(disambiguation)" title="Earthquake (disambiguation)">Earthquake (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Seismic event" redirects here. For seismic migration, see <a href="/wiki/Seismic_migration" title="Seismic migration">Seismic migration</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the current earthquake season, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_earthquakes_in_2022" title="List of earthquakes in 2022">List of earthquakes in 2022</a>.</div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Seismite" title="Seismite">Seismite</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">Seismology</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Earth_sciences" title="Portal:Earth sciences">Earth Sciences Portal</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Category:Earthquakes" title="Category:Earthquakes">Category</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_geology_articles" title="Index of geology articles">Related topics</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Earthquakes" title="Template:Earthquakes"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Earthquakes" title="Template talk:Earthquakes"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Earthquakes&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <b>earthquake</b> (also known as a <b>quake</b>, <b>tremor</b> or <b>temblor</b>) is the shaking of the surface of the Earth resulting from a sudden release of energy in the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> that creates <a href="/wiki/Seismic_wave" title="Seismic wave">seismic waves</a>. Earthquakes can range in intensity, from those that are so weak that they cannot be felt, to those violent enough to propel objects and people into the air and wreak destruction across entire cities. The <b>seismic activity</b> of an area is the frequency, type, and size of earthquakes experienced over a particular time period. The <a href="/wiki/Seismicity" title="Seismicity">seismicity</a> at a particular location in the Earth is the average rate of seismic energy release per unit volume.  The word <i>tremor</i> is also used for <a href="/wiki/Episodic_tremor_and_slip" title="Episodic tremor and slip">non-earthquake seismic rumbling</a>.
</p><p>At the Earth's surface, earthquakes manifest themselves by shaking and displacing or disrupting the ground. When the <a href="/wiki/Epicenter" title="Epicenter">epicenter</a> of a large earthquake is located offshore, the seabed may be displaced sufficiently to cause a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>. Earthquakes can also trigger <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>.
</p><p>In its most general sense, the word <i>earthquake</i> is used to describe any seismic eventâwhether natural or caused by humansâthat generates seismic waves. Earthquakes are caused mostly by rupture of geological <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faults</a> but also by other events such as volcanic activity, landslides, mine blasts, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Underground_nuclear_testing" title="Underground nuclear testing">nuclear tests</a>. An earthquake's point of initial rupture is called its <a href="/wiki/Hypocenter" title="Hypocenter">hypocenter</a> or focus. The <a href="/wiki/Epicenter" title="Epicenter">epicenter</a> is the point at ground level directly above the hypocenter.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naturally_occurring_earthquakes">Naturally occurring earthquakes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fault_types.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="416" decoding="async" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fault_types.svg/220px-Fault_types.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fault_types.svg/330px-Fault_types.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fault_types.svg/440px-Fault_types.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fault_types.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three types of faults:<br/> A. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strike-slip_fault" title="Strike-slip fault">Strike-slip</a><br/> B. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_fault" title="Normal fault">Normal</a><br/> C. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_fault" title="Reverse fault">Reverse</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tectonic earthquakes occur anywhere in the earth where there is sufficient stored elastic strain energy to drive fracture propagation along a <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">fault plane</a>. The sides of a fault move past each other smoothly and <a href="/wiki/Aseismic_creep" title="Aseismic creep">aseismically</a> only if there are no irregularities or <a href="/wiki/Asperity_(faults)" title="Asperity (faults)">asperities</a> along the fault surface that increase the frictional resistance. Most fault surfaces do have such asperities, which leads to a form of <a href="/wiki/Stick-slip_phenomenon" title="Stick-slip phenomenon">stick-slip behavior</a>. Once the fault has locked, continued relative motion between the plates leads to increasing stress and, therefore, stored strain energy in the volume around the fault surface. This continues until the stress has risen sufficiently to break through the asperity, suddenly allowing sliding over the locked portion of the fault, releasing the <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">stored energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohnaka_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ohnaka-1">[1]</a></sup> This energy is released as a combination of radiated elastic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strain_(materials_science)" title="Strain (materials science)">strain</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_waves" title="Seismic waves">seismic waves</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> frictional heating of the fault surface, and cracking of the rock, thus causing an earthquake. This process of gradual build-up of strain and stress punctuated by occasional sudden earthquake failure is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Elastic-rebound_theory" title="Elastic-rebound theory">elastic-rebound theory</a>. It is estimated that only 10 percent or less of an earthquake's total energy is radiated as seismic energy. Most of the earthquake's energy is used to power the earthquake <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(geology)" title="Fracture (geology)">fracture</a> growth or is converted into heat generated by friction. Therefore, earthquakes lower the Earth's available <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_potential_energy" title="Elastic potential energy">elastic potential energy</a> and raise its temperature, though these changes are negligible compared to the conductive and convective flow of heat out from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">Earth's deep interior.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquake_fault_types">Earthquake fault types</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">Fault (geology)</a></div>
<p>There are three main types of fault, all of which may cause an <a href="/wiki/Interplate_earthquake" title="Interplate earthquake">interplate earthquake</a>: normal, reverse (thrust), and strike-slip. Normal and reverse faulting are examples of dip-slip, where the displacement along the fault is in the direction of <a href="/wiki/Strike_and_dip" title="Strike and dip">dip</a> and where movement on them involves a vertical component. Normal faults occur mainly in areas where the crust is being <a href="/wiki/Extensional_tectonics" title="Extensional tectonics">extended</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundary</a>. Reverse faults occur in areas where the crust is being <a href="/wiki/Thrust_tectonics" title="Thrust tectonics">shortened</a> such as at a convergent boundary. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strike-slip_fault" title="Strike-slip fault">Strike-slip faults</a> are steep structures where the two sides of the fault slip horizontally past each other; transform boundaries are a particular type of strike-slip fault. Many earthquakes are caused by movement on faults that have components of both dip-slip and strike-slip; this is known as oblique slip.
</p><p>Reverse faults, particularly those along <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent plate boundaries</a>, are associated with the most powerful earthquakes, <a href="/wiki/Megathrust_earthquake" title="Megathrust earthquake">megathrust earthquakes</a>, including almost all of those of magnitude 8 or more. Megathrust earthquakes are responsible for about 90% of the total seismic moment released worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Strike-slip faults, particularly continental <a href="/wiki/Transform_fault" title="Transform fault">transforms</a>, can produce major earthquakes up to about magnitude 8. Earthquakes associated with normal faults are generally less than magnitude 7. For every unit increase in magnitude, there is a roughly thirtyfold increase in the energy released. For instance, an earthquake of magnitude 6.0 releases approximately 32 times more energy than a 5.0 magnitude earthquake and a 7.0 magnitude earthquake releases 1,000 times more energy than a 5.0 magnitude earthquake. An 8.6 magnitude earthquake releases the same amount of energy as 10,000 atomic bombs of the size used in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>This is so because the energy released in an earthquake, and thus its magnitude, is proportional to the area of the fault that ruptures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and the stress drop. Therefore, the longer the length and the wider the width of the faulted area, the larger the resulting magnitude. The topmost, brittle part of the Earth's crust, and the cool slabs of the tectonic plates that are descending into the hot mantle, are the only parts of our planet that can store elastic energy and release it in fault ruptures. Rocks hotter than about 300Â Â°C (572Â Â°F) flow in response to stress; they do not rupture in earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The maximum observed lengths of ruptures and mapped faults (which may break in a single rupture) are approximately 1,000Â km (620Â mi). Examples are the earthquakes in <a href="/wiki/1957_Andreanof_Islands_earthquake" title="1957 Andreanof Islands earthquake">Alaska (1957)</a>, <a href="/wiki/1960_Valdivia_earthquake" title="1960 Valdivia earthquake">Chile (1960)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake and tsunami">Sumatra (2004)</a>, all in subduction zones. The longest earthquake ruptures on strike-slip faults, like the <a href="/wiki/San_Andreas_Fault" title="San Andreas Fault">San Andreas Fault</a> (<a href="/wiki/1857_Fort_Tejon_earthquake" title="1857 Fort Tejon earthquake">1857</a>, <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906</a>), the <a href="/wiki/North_Anatolian_Fault" title="North Anatolian Fault">North Anatolian Fault</a> in Turkey (<a href="/wiki/1939_Erzincan_earthquake" title="1939 Erzincan earthquake">1939</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Denali_Fault" title="Denali Fault">Denali Fault</a> in Alaska (<a href="/wiki/2002_Denali_earthquake" title="2002 Denali earthquake">2002</a>), are about half to one third as long as the lengths along subducting plate margins, and those along normal faults are even shorter.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg/220px-Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg/330px-Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg/440px-Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kluft-photo-Carrizo-Plain-Nov-2007-Img_0327.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial photo of the San Andreas Fault in the <a href="/wiki/Carrizo_Plain" title="Carrizo Plain">Carrizo Plain</a>, northwest of Los Angeles</div></div></div>
<p>The most important parameter controlling the maximum earthquake magnitude on a fault, however, is not the maximum available length, but the available width because the latter varies by a factor of 20. Along converging plate margins, the dip angle of the rupture plane is very shallow, typically about 10 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Thus, the width of the plane within the top brittle crust of the Earth can become 50â100Â km (31â62Â mi) (<a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 TÅhoku earthquake and tsunami">Japan, 2011</a>; <a href="/wiki/1964_Alaska_earthquake" title="1964 Alaska earthquake">Alaska, 1964</a>), making the most powerful earthquakes possible.
</p><p>Strike-slip faults tend to be oriented near vertically, resulting in an approximate width of 10Â km (6.2Â mi) within the brittle crust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Thus, earthquakes with magnitudes much larger than 8 are not possible. Maximum magnitudes along many normal faults are even more limited because many of them are located along spreading centers, as in Iceland, where the thickness of the brittle layer is only about six kilometres (3.7Â mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, there exists a hierarchy of stress levels in the three fault types. Thrust faults are generated by the highest, strike-slip by intermediate, and normal faults by the lowest stress levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> This can easily be understood by considering the direction of the greatest principal stress, the direction of the force that "pushes" the rock mass during the faulting. In the case of normal faults, the rock mass is pushed down in a vertical direction, thus the pushing force (<i>greatest</i> principal stress) equals the weight of the rock mass itself. In the case of thrusting, the rock mass "escapes" in the direction of the least principal stress, namely upward, lifting the rock mass, and thus, the overburden equals the <i>least</i> principal stress. Strike-slip faulting is intermediate between the other two types described above. This difference in stress regime in the three faulting environments can contribute to differences in stress drop during faulting, which contributes to differences in the radiated energy, regardless of fault dimensions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquakes_away_from_plate_boundaries">Earthquakes away from plate boundaries</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Intraplate_earthquake" title="Intraplate earthquake">Intraplate earthquake</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tremor(English).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="836" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Tremor%28English%29.gif/220px-Tremor%28English%29.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Tremor%28English%29.gif/330px-Tremor%28English%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Tremor%28English%29.gif/440px-Tremor%28English%29.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tremor(English).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of the <a href="/wiki/1985_Mexico_City_earthquake" title="1985 Mexico City earthquake">1985</a> and <a href="/wiki/2017_Puebla_earthquake" title="2017 Puebla earthquake">2017</a> earthquakes on Mexico City, Puebla and MichoacÃ¡n/Guerrero</div></div></div>
<p>Where plate boundaries occur within the <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental lithosphere</a>, deformation is spread out over a much larger area than the plate boundary itself. In the case of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Andreas_fault" title="San Andreas fault">San Andreas fault</a> continental transform, many earthquakes occur away from the plate boundary and are related to strains developed within the broader zone of deformation caused by major irregularities in the fault trace (e.g., the "Big bend" region). The <a href="/wiki/1994_Northridge_earthquake" title="1994 Northridge earthquake">Northridge earthquake</a> was associated with movement on a blind thrust within such a zone. Another example is the strongly oblique convergent plate boundary between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_plate" title="Arabian plate">Arabian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian_plate" title="Eurasian plate">Eurasian plates</a> where it runs through the northwestern part of the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a>. The deformation associated with this plate boundary is partitioned into nearly pure thrust sense movements perpendicular to the boundary over a wide zone to the southwest and nearly pure strike-slip motion along the Main Recent Fault close to the actual plate boundary itself. This is demonstrated by earthquake <a href="/wiki/Focal_mechanism" title="Focal mechanism">focal mechanisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>All tectonic plates have internal stress fields caused by their interactions with neighboring plates and sedimentary loading or unloading (e.g., deglaciation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> These stresses may be sufficient to cause failure along existing fault planes, giving rise to intraplate earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noson_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noson-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shallow-focus_and_deep-focus_earthquakes">Shallow-focus and deep-focus earthquakes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depth_of_focus_(tectonics)" title="Depth of focus (tectonics)">Depth of focus (tectonics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HotelSanSalvador.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="403" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HotelSanSalvador.jpg/220px-HotelSanSalvador.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HotelSanSalvador.jpg/330px-HotelSanSalvador.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/HotelSanSalvador.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HotelSanSalvador.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Collapsed Gran Hotel building in the <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador" title="San Salvador">San Salvador</a> metropolis, after the shallow <a href="/wiki/1986_San_Salvador_earthquake" title="1986 San Salvador earthquake">1986 San Salvador earthquake</a></div></div></div>
<p>The majority of tectonic earthquakes originate in the ring of fire at depths not exceeding tens of kilometers. Earthquakes occurring at a depth of less than 70Â km (43Â mi) are classified as "shallow-focus" earthquakes, while those with a focal-depth between 70 and 300Â km (43 and 186Â mi) are commonly termed "mid-focus" or "intermediate-depth" earthquakes. In <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">Subduction</a> zones, where older and colder <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a> descends beneath another tectonic plate, <a href="/wiki/Deep-focus_earthquake" title="Deep-focus earthquake">deep-focus earthquakes</a> may occur at much greater depths (ranging from 300 to 700Â km (190 to 430Â mi)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> These seismically active areas of subduction are known as <a href="/wiki/Wadati%E2%80%93Benioff_zone" title="WadatiâBenioff zone">WadatiâBenioff zones</a>. Deep-focus earthquakes occur at a depth where the subducted <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> should no longer be brittle, due to the high temperature and pressure. A possible mechanism for the generation of deep-focus earthquakes is faulting caused by <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> undergoing a <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Spinel" title="Spinel">spinel</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olivine_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-olivine-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquakes_and_volcanic_activity">Earthquakes and volcanic activity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Volcano_tectonic_earthquake" title="Volcano tectonic earthquake">Volcano tectonic earthquake</a></div>
<p>Earthquakes often occur in volcanic regions and are caused there, both by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plates" title="Tectonic plates">tectonic</a> faults and the movement of <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> in <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>. Such earthquakes can serve as an early warning of volcanic eruptions, as during the <a href="/wiki/1980_eruption_of_Mount_St._Helens" title="1980 eruption of Mount St. Helens">1980 eruption of Mount St. Helens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Earthquake swarms can serve as markers for the location of the flowing magma throughout the volcanoes. These swarms can be recorded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismometers" title="Seismometers">seismometers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiltmeter" title="Tiltmeter">tiltmeters</a> (a device that measures ground slope) and used as sensors to predict imminent or upcoming eruptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rupture_dynamics">Rupture dynamics</span></h3>
<p>A tectonic earthquake begins by an initial rupture at a point on the fault surface, a process known as nucleation. The scale of the nucleation zone is uncertain, with some evidence, such as the rupture dimensions of the smallest earthquakes, suggesting that it is smaller than 100Â m (330Â ft) while other evidence, such as a slow component revealed by low-frequency spectra of some earthquakes, suggest that it is larger. The possibility that the nucleation involves some sort of preparation process is supported by the observation that about 40% of earthquakes are preceded by foreshocks. Once the rupture has initiated, it begins to propagate along the fault surface. The mechanics of this process are poorly understood, partly because it is difficult to recreate the high sliding velocities in a laboratory. Also, the effects of strong ground motion make it very difficult to record information close to a nucleation zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRS_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRS-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rupture propagation is generally modeled using a <a href="/wiki/Fracture_mechanics" title="Fracture mechanics">fracture mechanics</a> approach, likening the rupture to a propagating mixed mode shear crack. The rupture velocity is a function of the fracture energy in the volume around the crack tip, increasing with decreasing fracture energy. The velocity of rupture propagation is orders of magnitude faster than the displacement velocity across the fault. Earthquake ruptures typically propagate at velocities that are in the range 70â90% of the S-wave velocity, which is independent of earthquake size. A small subset of earthquake ruptures appear to have propagated at speeds greater than the S-wave velocity. These <a href="/wiki/Supershear_earthquake" title="Supershear earthquake">supershear earthquakes</a> have all been observed during large strike-slip events. The unusually wide zone of coseismic damage caused by the <a href="/wiki/2001_Kunlun_earthquake" title="2001 Kunlun earthquake">2001 Kunlun earthquake</a> has been attributed to the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">sonic boom</a> developed in such earthquakes. Some earthquake ruptures travel at unusually low velocities and are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Slow_earthquake" title="Slow earthquake">slow earthquakes</a>. A particularly dangerous form of slow earthquake is the <a href="/wiki/Tsunami_earthquake" title="Tsunami earthquake">tsunami earthquake</a>, observed where the relatively low felt intensities, caused by the slow propagation speed of some great earthquakes, fail to alert the population of the neighboring coast, as in the <a href="/wiki/1896_Sanriku_earthquake" title="1896 Sanriku earthquake">1896 Sanriku earthquake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRS_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-NRS-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Co-seismic_overpressuring_and_effect_of_pore_pressure">Co-seismic overpressuring and effect of pore pressure</span></h4>
<p>During an earthquake, high temperatures can develop at the fault plane so increasing pore pressure consequently to vaporization of the ground water already contained within rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sibson_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sibson-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudnicki_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudnicki-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guerriero_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guerriero-24">[24]</a></sup> 
In the coseismic phase, such increase can significantly affect slip evolution and speed and, furthermore, in the post-seismic phase it can control the <a href="/wiki/Aftershock" title="Aftershock">Aftershock</a> sequence  because, after the main event, pore pressure increase slowly propagates into the surrounding fracture network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nur_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nur-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guerriero_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guerriero-24">[24]</a></sup>
From the point of view of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohr-Coulomb_theory" title="Mohr-Coulomb theory">Mohr-Coulomb strength theory</a>, an increase in fluid pressure reduces the normal stress acting on the fault plane that holds it in place, and fluids can exert a lubricating effect.
As thermal overpressurization may provide positive feedback between slip and strength fall at the fault plane, a common opinion is that it may enhance the faulting process instability. After the mainshock, the pressure gradient between the fault plane and the neighboring rock causes a fluid flow which increases pore pressure in the surrounding fracture networks; such increase may trigger new faulting processes by reactivating adjacent faults, giving rise to aftershocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nur_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nur-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guerriero_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guerriero-24">[24]</a></sup> Analogously, artificial pore pressure increase, by fluid injection in Earth's crust, may <a href="/wiki/Induced_seismicity" title="Induced seismicity">induce seismicity</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tidal_forces">Tidal forces</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_triggering_of_earthquakes" title="Tidal triggering of earthquakes">Tidal triggering of earthquakes</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tides" title="Tides">Tides</a> may induce some <a href="/wiki/Seismicity" title="Seismicity">seismicity</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquake_clusters">Earthquake clusters</span></h3>
<p>Most earthquakes form part of a sequence, related to each other in terms of location and time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAAFEC_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-WAAFEC-26">[26]</a></sup> Most earthquake clusters consist of small tremors that cause little to no damage, but there is a theory that earthquakes can recur in a regular pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftershocks">Aftershocks</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aftershock" title="Aftershock">Aftershock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg/250px-2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg/375px-2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg/500px-2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2016_Central_Italy_earthquake_wide.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Magnitude of the <a href="/wiki/August_2016_Central_Italy_earthquake" title="August 2016 Central Italy earthquake">Central Italy earthquakes of August</a> and <a href="/wiki/October_2016_Central_Italy_earthquakes" title="October 2016 Central Italy earthquakes">October 2016</a> and <a href="/wiki/January_2017_Central_Italy_earthquakes" title="January 2017 Central Italy earthquakes">January 2017</a> and the aftershocks (which continued to occur after the period shown here)</div></div></div>
<p>An aftershock is an earthquake that occurs after a previous earthquake, the mainshock. Rapid changes of stress between rocks, and the stress from the original earthquake are the main causes of these aftershocks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-28">[28]</a></sup> along with the crust around the ruptured <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">fault plane</a> as it adjusts to the effects of the mainshock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAAFEC_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-WAAFEC-26">[26]</a></sup> An aftershock is in the same region of the main shock but always of a smaller magnitude, however they can still be powerful enough to cause even more damage to buildings that were already previously damaged from the original quake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-28">[28]</a></sup> If an aftershock is larger than the mainshock, the aftershock is redesignated as the mainshock and the original main shock is redesignated as a <a href="/wiki/Foreshock" title="Foreshock">foreshock</a>. Aftershocks are formed as the crust around the displaced <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">fault plane</a> adjusts to the effects of the mainshock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAAFEC_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-WAAFEC-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquake_swarms">Earthquake swarms</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_swarm" title="Earthquake swarm">Earthquake swarm</a></div>
<p>Earthquake swarms are sequences of earthquakes striking in a specific area within a short period. They are different from earthquakes followed by a series of <a href="/wiki/Aftershock" title="Aftershock">aftershocks</a> by the fact that no single earthquake in the sequence is obviously the main shock, so none has a notable higher magnitude than another. An example of an earthquake swarm is the 2004 activity at <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In August 2012, a swarm of earthquakes shook <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">Southern California</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Valley" title="Imperial Valley">Imperial Valley</a>, showing the most recorded activity in the area since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes a series of earthquakes occur in what has been called an <i>earthquake storm</i>, where the earthquakes strike a fault in clusters, each triggered by the shaking or <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_stress_transfer" title="Coulomb stress transfer">stress redistribution</a> of the previous earthquakes. Similar to <a href="/wiki/Aftershock" title="Aftershock">aftershocks</a> but on adjacent segments of fault, these storms occur over the course of years, and with some of the later earthquakes as damaging as the early ones. Such a pattern was observed in the sequence of about a dozen earthquakes that struck the <a href="/wiki/North_Anatolian_Fault" title="North Anatolian Fault">North Anatolian Fault</a> in Turkey in the 20th century and has been inferred for older anomalous clusters of large earthquakes in the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity_of_earth_quaking_and_magnitude_of_earthquakes"><span class="anchor" id="Magnitude"></span>Intensity of earth quaking and magnitude of earthquakes</span></h2>
<p>Quaking or shaking of the earth is a common phenomenon undoubtedly known to humans from the earliest times. Before the development of <a href="/wiki/Accelerograph" title="Accelerograph">strong-motion accelerometers</a> that can measure peak ground speed and acceleration directly, the intensity of the earth-shaking was estimated based on the observed effects, as categorized on various <a href="/wiki/Seismic_intensity_scales" title="Seismic intensity scales">seismic intensity scales</a>. Only in the last century has the source of such shaking been identified as ruptures in the Earth's crust, with the intensity of shaking at any locality dependent not only on the local ground conditions but also on the strength or <i>magnitude</i> of the rupture, and on its distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Seismic_magnitude_scales#Richter" title="Seismic magnitude scales">first scale for measuring earthquake magnitudes</a> was developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_F._Richter" title="Charles F. Richter">Charles F. Richter</a> in 1935. Subsequent scales (see <a href="/wiki/Seismic_magnitude_scales" title="Seismic magnitude scales">seismic magnitude scales</a>) have retained a key feature, where each unit represents a ten-fold difference in the amplitude of the ground shaking and a 32-fold difference in energy. Subsequent scales are also adjusted to have approximately the same numeric value within the limits of the scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the mass media commonly reports earthquake magnitudes as "Richter magnitude" or "Richter scale", standard practice by most seismological authorities is to express an earthquake's strength on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_scale#Mw" title="Seismic scale">moment magnitude</a> scale, which is based on the actual energy released by an earthquake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequency_of_occurrence">Frequency of occurrence</span></h2>
<p>It is estimated that around 500,000 earthquakes occur each year, detectable with current instrumentation. About 100,000 of these can be felt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgsfacts_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgsfacts-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp100414_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-wp100414-37">[37]</a></sup> Minor earthquakes occur nearly constantly around the world in places like California and Alaska in the U.S., as well as in El Salvador, Mexico, Guatemala, Chile, Peru, Indonesia, the Philippines, Iran, Pakistan, the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> in Portugal, Turkey, New Zealand, Greece, Italy, India, Nepal and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Larger earthquakes occur less frequently, the relationship being <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg%E2%80%93Richter_law" title="GutenbergâRichter law">exponential</a>; for example, roughly ten times as many earthquakes larger than magnitude 4 occur in a particular time period than earthquakes larger than magnitude 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In the (low seismicity) United Kingdom, for example, it has been calculated that the average recurrences are:
an earthquake of 3.7â4.6 every year, an earthquake of 4.7â5.5 every 10Â years, and an earthquake of 5.6 or larger every 100Â years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This is an example of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg%E2%80%93Richter_law" title="GutenbergâRichter law">GutenbergâRichter law</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comerio,_Luca_(1878-1940)_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_(dicembre_1908).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Comerio%2C_Luca_%281878-1940%29_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_%28dicembre_1908%29.jpg/220px-Comerio%2C_Luca_%281878-1940%29_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_%28dicembre_1908%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Comerio%2C_Luca_%281878-1940%29_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_%28dicembre_1908%29.jpg/330px-Comerio%2C_Luca_%281878-1940%29_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_%28dicembre_1908%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Comerio%2C_Luca_%281878-1940%29_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_%28dicembre_1908%29.jpg/440px-Comerio%2C_Luca_%281878-1940%29_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_%28dicembre_1908%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comerio,_Luca_(1878-1940)_-_Vittime_del_terremoto_di_Messina_(dicembre_1908).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/1908_Messina_earthquake" title="1908 Messina earthquake">Messina earthquake</a> and tsunami took as many as 200,000 lives on December 28, 1908, in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The number of seismic stations has increased from about 350 in 1931 to many thousands today. As a result, many more earthquakes are reported than in the past, but this is because of the vast improvement in instrumentation, rather than an increase in the number of earthquakes. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> (USGS) estimates that, since 1900, there have been an average of 18 major earthquakes (magnitude 7.0â7.9) and one great earthquake (magnitude 8.0 or greater) per year, and that this average has been relatively stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In recent years, the number of major earthquakes per year has decreased, though this is probably a statistical fluctuation rather than a systematic trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> More detailed statistics on the size and frequency of earthquakes is available from the United States Geological Survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
A recent increase in the number of major earthquakes has been noted, which could be explained by a cyclical pattern of periods of intense tectonic activity, interspersed with longer periods of low intensity. However, accurate recordings of earthquakes only began in the early 1900s, so it is too early to categorically state that this is the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the world's earthquakes (90%, and 81% of the largest) take place in the 40,000-kilometre-long (25,000Â mi), horseshoe-shaped zone called the circum-Pacific seismic belt, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Ring_of_Fire" title="Pacific Ring of Fire">Pacific Ring of Fire</a>, which for the most part bounds the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific Plate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Massive earthquakes tend to occur along other plate boundaries too, such as along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalayan_Mountains" title="Himalayan Mountains">Himalayan Mountains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the rapid growth of <a href="/wiki/Megacity" title="Megacity">mega-cities</a> such as Mexico City, Tokyo and Tehran in areas of high <a href="/wiki/Seismic_risk" title="Seismic risk">seismic risk</a>, some seismologists are warning that a single quake may claim the lives of up to three million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Induced_seismicity">Induced seismicity</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Induced_seismicity" title="Induced seismicity">Induced seismicity</a></div>
<p>While most earthquakes are caused by movement of the Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plates</a>, human activity can also produce earthquakes. Activities both above ground and below may change the stresses and strains on the crust, including building reservoirs, extracting resources such as coal or oil, and injecting fluids underground for waste disposal or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fracking" title="Fracking">fracking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Most of these earthquakes have small magnitudes. The 5.7 magnitude <a href="/wiki/2011_Oklahoma_earthquake" title="2011 Oklahoma earthquake">2011 Oklahoma earthquake</a> is thought to have been caused by disposing wastewater from oil production into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injection_wells" title="Injection wells">injection wells</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and studies point to the state's oil industry as the cause of other earthquakes in the past century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> paper suggested that the 8.0 magnitude <a href="/wiki/2008_Sichuan_earthquake" title="2008 Sichuan earthquake">2008 Sichuan earthquake</a> was induced by loading from the <a href="/wiki/Zipingpu_Dam" title="Zipingpu Dam">Zipingpu Dam</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> though the link has not been conclusively proved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measuring_and_locating_earthquakes">Measuring and locating earthquakes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Seismic_magnitude_scales" title="Seismic magnitude scales">Seismic magnitude scales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">Seismology</a></div>
<p>The instrumental scales used to describe the size of an earthquake began with the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter magnitude scale</a> in the 1930s. It is a relatively simple measurement of an event's amplitude, and its use has become minimal in the 21st century. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_waves" title="Seismic waves">Seismic waves</a> travel through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_interior" title="Earth's interior">Earth's interior</a> and can be recorded by <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">seismometers</a> at great distances. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_wave_magnitude" title="Surface wave magnitude">surface wave magnitude</a> was developed in the 1950s as a means to measure remote earthquakes and to improve the accuracy for larger events. The <a href="/wiki/Moment_magnitude_scale" title="Moment magnitude scale">moment magnitude scale</a> not only measures the amplitude of the shock but also takes into account the <a href="/wiki/Seismic_moment" title="Seismic moment">seismic moment</a> (total rupture area, average slip of the fault, and rigidity of the rock). The <a href="/wiki/Japan_Meteorological_Agency_seismic_intensity_scale" title="Japan Meteorological Agency seismic intensity scale">Japan Meteorological Agency seismic intensity scale</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Medvedev%E2%80%93Sponheuer%E2%80%93Karnik_scale" title="MedvedevâSponheuerâKarnik scale">MedvedevâSponheuerâKarnik scale</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercalli_intensity_scale" title="Mercalli intensity scale">Mercalli intensity scale</a> are based on the observed effects and are related to the intensity of shaking.
</p><p>Every tremor produces different types of seismic waves, which travel through rock with different velocities:
</p>
<ul><li>Longitudinal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-waves" title="P-waves">P-waves</a> (shock- or pressure waves)</li>
<li>Transverse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-waves" title="S-waves">S-waves</a> (both body waves)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Surface_wave" title="Surface wave">Surface waves</a> â (<a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_wave" title="Rayleigh wave">Rayleigh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Love_wave" title="Love wave">Love</a> waves)</li></ul>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propagation_velocity" title="Propagation velocity">Propagation velocity</a> of the seismic waves through solid rock ranges from approx. 3Â km/s (1.9Â mi/s) up to 13Â km/s (8.1Â mi/s), depending on the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elasticity</a> of the medium. In the Earth's interior, the shock- or P-waves travel much faster than the S-waves (approx. relation 1.7:1). The differences in travel time from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicentre" title="Epicentre">epicenter</a> to the observatory are a measure of the distance and can be used to image both sources of quakes and structures within the Earth. Also, the depth of the <a href="/wiki/Hypocenter" title="Hypocenter">hypocenter</a> can be computed roughly.
</p><p>In the upper crust, P-waves travel in the range 2â3Â km (1.2â1.9Â mi) per second (or lower) in soils and unconsolidated sediments, increasing to 3â6Â km (1.9â3.7Â mi) per second in solid rock. In the lower crust, they travel at about 6â7Â km (3.7â4.3Â mi) per second; the velocity increases within the deep mantle to about 13Â km (8.1Â mi) per second. The velocity of S-waves ranges from 2â3Â km (1.2â1.9Â mi) per second in light sediments and 4â5Â km (2.5â3.1Â mi) per second in the Earth's crust up to 7Â km (4.3Â mi) per second in the deep mantle. As a consequence, the first waves of a distant earthquake arrive at an observatory via the Earth's mantle.
</p><p>On average, the kilometer distance to the earthquake is the number of seconds between the P- and S-wave <b>times 8</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Slight deviations are caused by inhomogeneities of subsurface structure. By such analysis of seismograms, the Earth's core was located in 1913 by <a href="/wiki/Beno_Gutenberg" title="Beno Gutenberg">Beno Gutenberg</a>.
</p><p>S-waves and later arriving surface waves do most of the damage compared to P-waves. P-waves squeeze and expand the material in the same direction they are traveling, whereas S-waves shake the ground up and down and back and forth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earthquakes are not only categorized by their magnitude but also by the place where they occur. The world is divided into 754 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flinn%E2%80%93Engdahl_regions" title="FlinnâEngdahl regions">FlinnâEngdahl regions</a> (F-E regions), which are based on political and geographical boundaries as well as seismic activity. More active zones are divided into smaller F-E regions whereas less active zones belong to larger F-E regions.
</p><p>Standard reporting of earthquakes includes its <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">magnitude</a>, date and time of occurrence, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinates" title="Geographic coordinates">geographic coordinates</a> of its <a href="/wiki/Epicenter" title="Epicenter">epicenter</a>, depth of the epicenter, geographical region, distances to population centers, location uncertainty, several parameters that are included in USGS earthquake reports (number of stations reporting, number of observations, etc.), and a unique event ID.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although relatively slow seismic waves have traditionally been used to detect earthquakes, scientists realized in 2016 that gravitational measurements could provide instantaneous detection of earthquakes, and confirmed this by analyzing gravitational records associated with the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 TÅhoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tohoku-Oki</a> ("Fukushima") earthquake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_earthquakes">Effects of earthquakes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="998" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg/220px-1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg/330px-1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg/440px-1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1755_Lisbon_earthquake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1755 copper engraving depicting <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> in ruins and in flames after the <a href="/wiki/1755_Lisbon_earthquake" title="1755 Lisbon earthquake">1755 Lisbon earthquake</a>, which killed an estimated 60,000 people. A <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> overwhelms the ships in the harbor.</div></div></div>
<p>The effects of earthquakes include, but are not limited to, the following:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaking_and_ground_rupture">Shaking and ground rupture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg/220px-Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg/330px-Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg/440px-Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haiti_earthquake_damage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Damaged buildings in <a href="/wiki/Port-au-Prince" title="Port-au-Prince">Port-au-Prince</a>, <a href="/wiki/2010_Haiti_earthquake" title="2010 Haiti earthquake">Haiti</a>, January 2010.</div></div></div>
<p>Shaking and <a href="/wiki/Surface_rupture" title="Surface rupture">ground rupture</a> are the main effects created by earthquakes, principally resulting in more or less severe damage to buildings and other rigid structures. The severity of the local effects depends on the complex combination of the earthquake <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">magnitude</a>, the distance from the <a href="/wiki/Epicenter" title="Epicenter">epicenter</a>, and the local geological and geomorphological conditions, which may amplify or reduce <a href="/wiki/Wave_propagation" title="Wave propagation">wave propagation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The ground-shaking is measured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_acceleration" title="Ground acceleration">ground acceleration</a>.
</p><p>Specific local geological, geomorphological, and geostructural features can induce high levels of shaking on the ground surface even from low-intensity earthquakes. This effect is called site or local amplification. It is principally due to the transfer of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic" title="Seismic">seismic</a> motion from hard deep soils to soft superficial soils and the effects of seismic energy focalization owing to the typical geometrical setting of such deposits.
</p><p>Ground rupture is a visible breaking and displacement of the Earth's surface along the trace of the fault, which may be of the order of several meters in the case of major earthquakes. Ground rupture is a major risk for large engineering structures such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dams" title="Dams">dams</a>, bridges, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_stations" title="Nuclear power stations">nuclear power stations</a> and requires careful mapping of existing faults to identify any that are likely to break the ground surface within the life of the structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil_liquefaction">Soil liquefaction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Soil_liquefaction" title="Soil liquefaction">Soil liquefaction</a></div>
<p>Soil liquefaction occurs when, because of the shaking, water-saturated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granular" title="Granular">granular</a> material (such as sand) temporarily loses its strength and transforms from a solid to a liquid. Soil liquefaction may cause rigid structures, like buildings and bridges, to tilt or sink into the liquefied deposits. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/1964_Alaska_earthquake" title="1964 Alaska earthquake">1964 Alaska earthquake</a>, soil liquefaction caused many buildings to sink into the ground, eventually collapsing upon themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_impacts">Human impacts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="639" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG/220px-Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG/330px-Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG/440px-Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghajn_Hadid_Tower_closer_view.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/G%C4%A7ajn_%C4%A6adid_Tower" title="GÄ§ajn Ä¦adid Tower">GÄ§ajn Ä¦adid Tower</a>, which collapsed during the <a href="/wiki/1856_Heraklion_earthquake" title="1856 Heraklion earthquake">1856 Heraklion earthquake</a></div></div></div>
<p>An earthquake may cause injury and loss of life, road and bridge damage, general <a href="/wiki/Property_damage" title="Property damage">property damage</a>, and collapse or destabilization (potentially leading to future collapse) of buildings. The aftermath may bring disease, lack of basic necessities, mental consequences such as panic attacks, depression to survivors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and higher insurance premiums.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landslides">Landslides</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">Landslide</a></div>
<p>Earthquakes can produce slope instability leading to landslides, a major geological hazard. Landslide danger may persist while emergency personnel are attempting rescue work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fires">Fires</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sfearthquake3b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1693" data-file-width="2269" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Sfearthquake3b.jpg/220px-Sfearthquake3b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Sfearthquake3b.jpg/330px-Sfearthquake3b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Sfearthquake3b.jpg/440px-Sfearthquake3b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sfearthquake3b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fires of the <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a></div></div></div>
<p>Earthquakes can cause fires by damaging <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electrical power</a> or gas lines. In the event of water mains rupturing and a loss of pressure, it may also become difficult to stop the spread of a fire once it has started. For example, more deaths in the <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a> were caused by fire than by the earthquake itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tsunami">Tsunami</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2004-tsunami.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1114" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/2004-tsunami.jpg/220px-2004-tsunami.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/2004-tsunami.jpg/330px-2004-tsunami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/2004-tsunami.jpg/440px-2004-tsunami.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2004-tsunami.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tsunami of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake">2004 Indian Ocean earthquake</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">Tsunami</a></div>
<p>Tsunamis are long-wavelength, long-period sea waves produced by the sudden or abrupt movement of large volumes of waterâincluding when an earthquake <a href="/wiki/Submarine_earthquake" title="Submarine earthquake">occurs at sea</a>. In the open ocean, the distance between wave crests can surpass 100 kilometres (62Â mi), and the wave periods can vary from five minutes to one hour. Such tsunamis travel 600â800Â kilometers per hour (373â497 miles per hour), depending on water depth. Large waves produced by an earthquake or a submarine landslide can overrun nearby coastal areas in a matter of minutes. Tsunamis can also travel thousands of kilometers across open ocean and wreak destruction on far shores hours after the earthquake that generated them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noson_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Noson-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ordinarily, subduction earthquakes under magnitude 7.5 do not cause tsunamis, although some instances of this have been recorded. Most destructive tsunamis are caused by earthquakes of magnitude 7.5 or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noson_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Noson-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floods">Floods</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">Flood</a></div>
<p>Floods may be secondary effects of earthquakes, if dams are damaged. Earthquakes may cause landslips to dam rivers, which collapse and cause floods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The terrain below the <a href="/wiki/Sarez_Lake" title="Sarez Lake">Sarez Lake</a> in Tajikistan is in danger of catastrophic flooding if the <a href="/wiki/Landslide_dam" title="Landslide dam">landslide dam</a> formed by the earthquake, known as the <a href="/wiki/Usoi_Dam" title="Usoi Dam">Usoi Dam</a>, were to fail during a future earthquake. Impact projections suggest the flood could affect roughly 5Â million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_earthquakes">Major earthquakes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg/400px-Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg/600px-Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg/800px-Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_earthquakes_1900-.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earthquakes (M6.0+) since 1900 through 2017</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg/400px-USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg/600px-USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg/800px-USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USGS_magnitude_8_earthquakes_since_1900.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earthquakes of magnitude 8.0 and greater from 1900 to 2018. The apparent 3D volumes of the bubbles are linearly proportional to their respective fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_earthquakes" title="Lists of earthquakes">Lists of earthquakes</a></div>
<p>One of the most devastating earthquakes in recorded history was the <a href="/wiki/1556_Shaanxi_earthquake" title="1556 Shaanxi earthquake">1556 Shaanxi earthquake</a>, which occurred on 23 January 1556 in <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, China. More than 830,000 people died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Most houses in the area were <a href="/wiki/Yaodong" title="Yaodong">yaodongs</a>âdwellings carved out of <a href="/wiki/Loess" title="Loess">loess</a> hillsidesâand many victims were killed when these structures collapsed. The <a href="/wiki/1976_Tangshan_earthquake" title="1976 Tangshan earthquake">1976 Tangshan earthquake</a>, which killed between 240,000 and 655,000 people, was the deadliest of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1960_Valdivia_earthquake" title="1960 Valdivia earthquake">1960 Chilean earthquake</a> is the largest earthquake that has been measured on a seismograph, reaching 9.5 magnitude on 22 May 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgsfacts_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgsfacts-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp100414_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-wp100414-37">[37]</a></sup> Its epicenter was near CaÃ±ete, Chile. The energy released was approximately twice that of the next most powerful earthquake, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_Friday_earthquake" title="Good Friday earthquake">Good Friday earthquake</a> (27 March 1964), which was centered in <a href="/wiki/Prince_William_Sound" title="Prince William Sound">Prince William Sound</a>, Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The ten largest recorded earthquakes have all been <a href="/wiki/Megathrust_earthquake" title="Megathrust earthquake">megathrust earthquakes</a>; however, of these ten, only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake">2004 Indian Ocean earthquake</a> is simultaneously one of the deadliest earthquakes in history.
</p><p>Earthquakes that caused the greatest loss of life, while powerful, were deadly because of their proximity to either heavily populated areas or the ocean, where earthquakes often create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsunamis" title="Tsunamis">tsunamis</a> that can devastate communities thousands of kilometers away. Regions most at risk for great loss of life include those where earthquakes are relatively rare but powerful, and poor regions with lax, unenforced, or nonexistent seismic building codes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prediction">Prediction</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_prediction" title="Earthquake prediction">Earthquake prediction</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earthquake_prediction" title="Earthquake prediction">Earthquake prediction</a> is a branch of the science of <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a> concerned with the specification of the time, location, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_scale" title="Seismic scale">magnitude</a> of future earthquakes within stated limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Many methods have been developed for predicting the time and place in which earthquakes will occur. Despite considerable research efforts by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismologist" title="Seismologist">seismologists</a>, scientifically reproducible predictions cannot yet be made to a specific day or month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ludwin_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ludwin-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecasting">Forecasting</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_forecasting" title="Earthquake forecasting">Earthquake forecasting</a></div>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Forecasting" title="Forecasting">forecasting</a> is usually considered to be a type of <a href="/wiki/Prediction" title="Prediction">prediction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_forecasting" title="Earthquake forecasting">earthquake forecasting</a> is often differentiated from <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_prediction" title="Earthquake prediction">earthquake prediction</a>. Earthquake forecasting is concerned with the probabilistic assessment of general earthquake hazard, including the frequency and magnitude of damaging earthquakes in a given area over years or decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> For well-understood faults the probability that a segment may rupture during the next few decades can be estimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Earthquake_warning_system" title="Earthquake warning system">Earthquake warning systems</a> have been developed that can provide regional notification of an earthquake in progress, but before the ground surface has begun to move, potentially allowing people within the system's range to seek shelter before the earthquake's impact is felt.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparedness">Preparedness</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_preparedness" title="Earthquake preparedness">Earthquake preparedness</a></div>
<p>The objective of <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_engineering" title="Earthquake engineering">earthquake engineering</a> is to foresee the impact of earthquakes on buildings and other structures and to design such structures to minimize the risk of damage. Existing structures can be modified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_retrofitting" title="Seismic retrofitting">seismic retrofitting</a> to improve their resistance to earthquakes. <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_insurance" title="Earthquake insurance">Earthquake insurance</a> can provide building owners with financial protection against losses resulting from earthquakes. <a href="/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">Emergency management</a> strategies can be employed by a government or organization to mitigate risks and prepare for consequences.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">Artificial intelligence</a> may help to assess buildings and plan precautionary operations: the Igor <a href="/wiki/Expert_system" title="Expert system">expert system</a> is part of a mobile laboratory that supports the procedures leading to the seismic assessment of masonry buildings and the planning of retrofitting operations on them. It has been successfully applied to assess buildings in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Individuals can also take preparedness steps like securing <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heaters</a> and heavy items that could injure someone, locating shutoffs for utilities, and being educated about what to do when the shaking starts. For areas near large bodies of water, earthquake preparedness encompasses the possibility of a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> caused by a large quake.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_views">Historical views</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="402" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg/220px-Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg/330px-Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lycosth%C3%A8ne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An image from a 1557 book depicting an earthquake in Italy in the 4th century BCE</div></div></div>
<p>From the lifetime of the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a> in the 5th century BCE to the 14th century CE, earthquakes were usually attributed to "air (vapors) in the cavities of the Earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-World-79">[79]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> of Miletus (625â547 BCE) was the only documented person who believed that earthquakes were caused by tension between the earth and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-World-79">[79]</a></sup> Other theories existed, including the Greek philosopher Anaxamines' (585â526 BCE) beliefs that short incline episodes of dryness and wetness caused seismic activity. The Greek philosopher Democritus (460â371 BCE) blamed water in general for earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-World-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> called earthquakes "underground thunderstorms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-World-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_studies">Recent studies</span></h2>
<p>In recent studies, geologists claim that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> is one of the reasons for increased seismic activity.
