According to these studies, melting glaciers and rising sea levels disturb the balance of pressure on Earth's tectonic plates, thus causing an increase in the frequency and intensity of earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2020)">betterÂ sourceÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology_and_religion">Mythology and religion</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, earthquakes were explained as the violent struggling of the god <a href="/wiki/Loki" title="Loki">Loki</a>. When Loki, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aesir" title="Aesir">god</a> of mischief and strife, murdered <a href="/wiki/Baldr" title="Baldr">Baldr</a>, god of beauty and light, he was punished by being bound in a cave with a poisonous serpent placed above his head dripping venom. Loki's wife <a href="/wiki/Sigyn" title="Sigyn">Sigyn</a> stood by him with a bowl to catch the poison, but whenever she had to empty the bowl the poison dripped on Loki's face, forcing him to jerk his head away and thrash against his bonds, which caused the earth to tremble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> was the cause and god of earthquakes. When he was in a bad mood, he struck the ground with a <a href="/wiki/Trident" title="Trident">trident</a>, causing earthquakes and other calamities. He also used earthquakes to punish and inflict fear upon people as revenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimock1990_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dimock1990-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Japanese_mythology" title="Japanese mythology">Japanese mythology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namazu_(Japanese_mythology)" title="Namazu (Japanese mythology)">Namazu</a> (é¯°) is a giant <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a> who causes earthquakes. Namazu lives in the mud beneath the earth and is guarded by the god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kashima_(god)" title="Kashima (god)">Kashima</a> who restrains the fish with a stone. When Kashima lets his guard fall, Namazu thrashes about, causing violent earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<p>In modern popular culture, the portrayal of earthquakes is shaped by the memory of great cities laid waste, such as <a href="/wiki/Great_Hanshin_earthquake" title="Great Hanshin earthquake">Kobe in 1995</a> or <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">San Francisco in 1906</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_60_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_60-84">[84]</a></sup> Fictional earthquakes tend to strike suddenly and without warning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_60_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_60-84">[84]</a></sup> For this reason, stories about earthquakes generally begin with the disaster and focus on its immediate aftermath, as in <i>Short Walk to Daylight</i> (1972), <i><a href="/wiki/A_Wrinkle_in_the_Skin" title="A Wrinkle in the Skin">The Ragged Edge</a></i> (1968) or <i><a href="/wiki/Aftershock:_Earthquake_in_New_York" title="Aftershock: Earthquake in New York">Aftershock: Earthquake in New York</a></i> (1999).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_60_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_60-84">[84]</a></sup> A notable example is Heinrich von Kleist's classic novella, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Earthquake_in_Chile" title="The Earthquake in Chile">The Earthquake in Chile</a></i>, which describes the destruction of Santiago in 1647. <a href="/wiki/Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami">Haruki Murakami</a>'s short fiction collection <i><a href="/wiki/After_the_Quake" title="After the Quake">After the Quake</a></i> depicts the consequences of the Kobe earthquake of 1995.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="big_one"></span>The most popular single earthquake in fiction is the hypothetical "Big One" expected of California's <a href="/wiki/San_Andreas_Fault" title="San Andreas Fault">San Andreas Fault</a> someday, as depicted in the novels <i><a href="/wiki/Richter_10" title="Richter 10">Richter 10</a></i> (1996), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goodbye_California_(novel)" title="Goodbye California (novel)">Goodbye California</a></i> (1977), <i><a href="/wiki/2012_(film)" title="2012 (film)">2012</a></i> (2009) and <i><a href="/wiki/San_Andreas_(film)" title="San Andreas (film)">San Andreas</a></i> (2015) among other works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Van_Riper_60_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Riper_60-84">[84]</a></sup> Jacob M. Appel's widely anthologized short story, <i>A Comparative Seismology</i>, features a con artist who convinces an elderly woman that an apocalyptic earthquake is imminent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary depictions of earthquakes in film are variable in the manner in which they reflect human psychological reactions to the actual trauma that can be caused to directly afflicted families and their loved ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Disaster mental health response research emphasizes the need to be aware of the different roles of loss of family and key community members, loss of home and familiar surroundings, loss of essential supplies and services to maintain survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Particularly for children, the clear availability of caregiving adults who are able to protect, nourish, and clothe them in the aftermath of the earthquake, and to help them make sense of what has befallen them has been shown even more important to their emotional and physical health than the simple giving of provisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> As was observed after other disasters involving destruction and loss of life and their media depictions, recently observed in the <a href="/wiki/2010_Haiti_earthquake" title="2010 Haiti earthquake">2010 Haiti earthquake</a>, it is also important not to pathologize the reactions to loss and displacement or disruption of governmental administration and services, but rather to validate these reactions, to support constructive problem-solving and reflection as to how one might improve the conditions of those affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>

"Observations of acute reactions of young children and their families to the World Trade Center attacks". <i>Journal of ZERO-TO-THREE: National Center for Infants, Toddlers, and Families</i>. <b>22</b> (3): 9â13.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+ZERO-TO-THREE%3A+National+Center+for+Infants%2C+Toddlers%2C+and+Families&amp;rft.atitle=Observations+of+acute+reactions+of+young+children+and+their+families+to+the+World+Trade+Center+attacks&amp;rft.volume=22&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=9-13&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Schechter&amp;rft.aufirst=DS&amp;rft.au=Coates%2C+SW&amp;rft.au=First%2C+E&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEarthquake"></span></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
